=== SOURCE CODE (py) ===
'''
# This is a class as personnel management system that implements functions such as adding, deleting, querying, and updating employees

class HRManagementSystem:
    def __init__(self):
        """
        Initialize the HRManagementSystem withan attribute employees, which is an empty dictionary.
        """
        self.employees = {}

    def add_employee(self, employee_id, name, position, department, salary):
        """
        Add a new employee to the HRManagementSystem.
        :param employee_id: The employee's id, int.
        :param name: The employee's name, str.
        :param position: The employee's position, str.
        :param department: The employee's department, str.
        :param salary: The employee's salary, int.
        :return: If the employee is already in the HRManagementSystem, returns False, otherwise, returns True.
        >>> hrManagementSystem = HRManagementSystem()
        >>> hrManagementSystem.add_employee(1, 'John', 'Manager', 'Sales', 100000)
        True
        >>> hrManagementSystem.add_employee(1, 'John', 'Manager', 'Sales', 100000)
        False

        """

    def remove_employee(self, employee_id):
        """
        Remove an employee from the HRManagementSystem.
        :param employee_id: The employee's id, int.
        :return: If the employee is already in the HRManagementSystem, returns True, otherwise, returns False.
        >>> hrManagementSystem = HRManagementSystem()
        >>> hrManagementSystem.employees = {1: {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}}
        >>> hrManagementSystem.remove_employee(1)
        True
        >>> hrManagementSystem.remove_employee(2)
        False

        """

    def update_employee(self, employee_id: int, employee_info: dict):
        """
        Update an employee's information in the HRManagementSystem.
        :param employee_id: The employee's id, int.
        :param employee_info: The employee's information, dict.
        :return: If the employee is already in the HRManagementSystem, returns True, otherwise, returns False.
        >>> hrManagementSystem = HRManagementSystem()
        >>> hrManagementSystem.employees = {1: {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}}
        >>> hrManagementSystem.update_employee(1, {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 20000})
        True
        >>> hrManagementSystem.update_employee(2, {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 20000})
        False

        """

    def get_employee(self, employee_id):
        """
        Get an employee's information from the HRManagementSystem.
        :param employee_id: The employee's id, int.
        :return: If the employee is already in the HRManagementSystem, returns the employee's information, otherwise, returns False.
        >>> hrManagementSystem = HRManagementSystem()
        >>> hrManagementSystem.employees = {1: {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}}
        >>> hrManagementSystem.get_employee(1)
        {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}
        >>> hrManagementSystem.get_employee(2)
        False

        """

    def list_employees(self):
        “”“
        List all employees' information in the HRManagementSystem.
        :return: A list of all employees' information,dict.
        >>> hrManagementSystem = HRManagementSystem()
        >>> hrManagementSystem.employees = {1: {'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}}
        >>> hrManagementSystem.list_employees()
        {1: {'employee_ID': 1, 'name': 'John', 'position': 'Manager', 'department': 'Sales', 'salary': 100000}}

        """

'''

class HRManagementSystem:
    def __init__(self):
        self.employees = {}

    def add_employee(self, employee_id, name, position, department, salary):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return False
        else:
            self.employees[employee_id] = {
                'name': name,
                'position': position,
                'department': department,
                'salary': salary
            }
            return True

    def remove_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False

    def update_employee(self, employee_id: int, employee_info: dict):
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True

    def get_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return self.employees[employee_id]
        else:
            return False

    def list_employees(self):
        employee_data = {}
        if self.employees:
            for employee_id, employee_info in self.employees.items():
                employee_details = {}
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id
                for key, value in employee_info.items():
                    employee_details[key] = value
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details
        return employee_data

```python
class HRManagementSystem:
    def __init__(self):
        self.employees = {}

    def add_employee(self, employee_id, name, position, department, salary):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return False
        else:
            self.employees[employee_id] = {
                'name': name,
                'position': position,
                'department': department,
                'salary': salary
            }
            return True

    def remove_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False

    def update_employee(self, employee_id: int, employee_info: dict):
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True

    def get_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return self.employees[employee_id]
        else:
            return False

    def list_employees(self):
        employee_data = {}
        if self.employees:
            for employee_id, employee_info in self.employees.items():
                employee_details = {}
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id
                for key, value in employee_info.items():
                    employee_details[key] = value
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details
        return employee_data
```

In C++, dictionaries are represented by `std::map` or `std::unordered_map`.
The employee info is a dictionary mapping strings to values. The values can be strings or integers.
Wait, `salary` is an integer. `employee_id` is an integer.

Let's use `std::variant<int, std::string>`. Wait, the order matters for `std::variant == std::variant`. If we use `std::variant<int, std::string>`, it will work.

```python
                if key not in employee:
```

`get_employee` returns a dictionary (which is truthy) or `False` (which is falsy).
In C++, returning a dictionary or `False` is tricky. We could return `std::optional<map>`, but `get_employee` returns the actual dictionary (by reference in Python, since modifying `employee` modifies `self.employees[employee_id]`).
Wait, `employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)` gets a reference to the dictionary in Python. Modifying `employee` modifies `self.employees`.
In C++, we can return a pointer to the internal map, or an iterator, or `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<...>>`.
If `employee_id` is not found, we can return `nullptr` or `std::nullopt`. But the Python code specifically returns `False`. In C++, we can't return `False` and a dictionary from the same function unless we use `std::variant` for the return type.
However, the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions)." But C++ is statically typed. A function returning either a dict or `bool` is hard to express. We can return `std::variant<std::map<std::string, Value>, bool>`, but that's very unidiomatic and cumbersome.

If we just implement `update_employee` directly in C++, we don't need `get_employee` to return `False`. We can just check if `employee_id` is in the map.

If we use `std::variant<std::map<std::string, Value>, bool>`, then `employee == False` becomes `std::holds_alternative<bool>(employee) && std::get<bool>(employee) == false`.

Let's see: "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."

```cpp
        if (it != employees.end()) {
```

Since `employee` is a reference to the internal dict, `employee[key] = value` updates the internal dict.
To replicate this in C++, `get_employee` should probably return a pointer to `EmployeeInfo`. A null pointer is equivalent to `False` (both are falsy), and a valid pointer is equivalent to the dict (both are truthy).
But the Python code specifically returns the boolean `False`.
If we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`, we can return `false` for the boolean, and a pointer to the dict for the dictionary.

```cpp
    std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool> get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        } else {
            return false;
        }
    }
```

```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        auto emp_var = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (std::holds_alternative<bool>(emp_var) && std::get<bool>(emp_var) == false) {
            return false;
        } else {
            EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_var);
            for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                    return false;
                }
            }
            for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                (*employee)[key] = value;
            }
            return true;
        }
    }
```
Wait, what if `get_employee` is called from outside? The user would get a `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`. If they expect to use the dict, they have to extract the pointer. This is very unidiomatic C++.

```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it == employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo& employee = it->second;
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee.find(key) == employee.end()) {
```

But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical". Does it mean we have to literally call `get_employee`? The Python code calls `get_employee`. If we don't call it, we change the internal implementation. But usually, "behavior identical" means *observable* behavior (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions).
Wait, `get_employee` is a public method. Its observable behavior is that it returns the employee's info if found, otherwise `False`.
In Python, `False` is often used as a substitute for `None` to indicate failure. In C++, the idiomatic way is `std::optional` or returning an iterator/pointer. If we use `std::optional<EmployeeInfo>`, returning `std::nullopt` is the C++ equivalent of returning `False` (both indicate absence/failure). But if the user explicitly checks `if (hr.get_employee(2) == false)`, `std::optional` doesn't compare equal to `false`.
However, C++ is statically typed. We can't return `false` and a map from the same function without `std::variant`.
Let's use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo, bool>` for `get_employee`.
Wait, if we return `EmployeeInfo` by value, it's a copy. The Python `get_employee` returns a reference to the dictionary. If the user modifies the returned dictionary in Python, it modifies the HR system's state. If we return by value in C++, modifying it won't affect the HR system. This changes behavior!
So we must return a pointer or reference.
`std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` is the closest we can get.
Wait, can we just return a pointer? `EmployeeInfo* get_employee(...)`. If not found, return `nullptr`.
In C++, `nullptr` is falsy when converted to `bool`, and a valid pointer is truthy. This perfectly matches the Python truthiness behavior of `get_employee`!
Let's check Python:
```python
employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
if employee == False:
    return False
```
If `get_employee` returns `nullptr`, `employee == false` would be `nullptr == false`, which is `true` in C++! Wait, `nullptr == false` is ill-formed in C++11 and later, or does it compile?
Actually, `nullptr == false` is a compile error in C++ because `nullptr` is `std::nullptr_t` and `false` is `bool`, and they are not comparable.
But if we use `get_employee` internally in `update_employee`, we can just check `if (!employee)`.
Wait, if `get_employee` is public, and the user writes C++ code like `auto res = hr.get_employee(1); if (res == false)`, it won't compile if `res` is a pointer.
What if we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`?
Then `res == false` would be `res == std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>(false)`, which evaluates to `true` if it holds `false`.
Let's check if `std::variant` has `==`.
Yes, `std::variant` supports `==`. If both hold the same alternative and the values are equal, it returns `true`. If they hold different alternatives, it returns `false`.
So `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>(false) == std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>(false)` is `true`.
`std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>(ptr) == std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>(false)` is `false`.
This exactly matches Python's `employee == False`!

Let's refine the types.
```cpp
#include <map>
#include <string>
#include <variant>

class HRManagementSystem {
public:
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `list_employees`, the Python code does:
```python
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id
```
Here, `employee_id` is an `int`. In our C++ `EmployeeInfo` (which is `std::map<std::string, Value>`), `Value` is `std::variant<int, std::string>`. So `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will correctly assign an `int` to the variant.

Let's double check `update_employee` in Python:
```python
    def update_employee(self, employee_id: int, employee_info: dict):
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If `employee_info` is empty, the loops don't execute, and it returns `True`. My C++ code handles this correctly.

Wait, is there any problem with returning `EmployeeInfo*` in `get_employee`?
If the user modifies the `HRManagementSystem` (e.g., adds/removes employees), the pointer might be invalidated if the internal `std::map` reallocates nodes. However, `std::map` iterators and pointers are not invalidated by insertions or deletions (except for the erased element). So returning a pointer to the value is safe in C++ as long as the element isn't erased.

What if `get_employee` is called and the returned pointer is used after the element is removed? That's undefined behavior in C++, but in Python it would also be an issue if a reference was held (though Python dicts don't return references that can be easily held and invalidated in the same way, but if you hold a reference to a dict, it stays alive even if removed from the HR system). In C++, if the element is removed, the pointer is dangling. This is a standard C++ lifetime issue, which is acceptable when translating from Python.

Wait, does `get_employee` return a reference to the *actual* dictionary in Python?
Yes, `self.employees[employee_id]` returns a reference to the dictionary. Any modifications to it are reflected in `self.employees`.
In C++, `&(it->second)` returns a pointer to the actual `EmployeeInfo` map. Modifying it through the pointer modifies the internal map. This matches Python's behavior perfectly.

Let's consider an alternative for `Value`. Instead of `std::variant<int, std::string>`, could we use `std::any`?
`std::any` is slower and requires casting, but it's more flexible. However, the Python code only uses `int` and `str` for values. `std::variant<int, std::string>` is type-safe and sufficient.

Let's review `list_employees`:
```python

```
In C++:
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
Wait, in Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Let's consider the `GetEmployeeResult` type. Is there a better way?
If we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`, the user has to include `<variant>`.
What if we just return `EmployeeInfo*` and use `nullptr` instead of `false`?
In Python, `get_employee` returns `False` if not found.
If we return `nullptr`, the behavior of `if (hr.get_employee(1) == false)` in C++ would be `if (nullptr == false)`, which is a compile error because `nullptr` is not comparable to `false`.
Wait, `if (hr.get_employee(1) == nullptr)` would work.
But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."
If we use `std::variant`, `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would be `hr.get_employee(1) == GetEmployeeResult(false)`, which compiles and behaves identically to Python.
However, returning a pointer to an internal map element is somewhat unidiomatic C++ (usually you'd return an iterator or `optional<reference_wrapper>`), but given the Python code returns a mutable reference or `False`, `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` is a very faithful translation.

Wait, could we use `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`?
If we return `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`, then `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would not compile because `std::optional` doesn't compare to `bool` with `==`. It can be contextually converted to `bool`, so `if (!hr.get_employee(1))` would work, but `== false` wouldn't.
In Python, `employee == False` is explicitly used in `update_employee`.
If we use `std::variant`, we can support `== false`.
Let's stick with `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`. It's the most accurate representation of the Python code's semantics.

Wait, is `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` safe?
If `EmployeeInfo*` happens to have the same value as `false` (i.e., `nullptr`), then `std::variant` holding `nullptr` would be different from `std::variant` holding `false`.
In Python, `False` is a boolean, not `None` or `0`. So `std::variant` distinguishes between `nullptr` (which would be like `None`) and `false` (which is `False`). This is perfect, because in Python, `get_employee` returns `False`, not `None`.

Let's check `update_employee` again.
```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }
```
This matches the Python logic perfectly.

Wait, in Python:
```python
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If `employee` is a dict, `employee == False` is `False`. So it goes to `else`.
In C++, `emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)` will be `false` if `emp_result` holds an `EmployeeInfo*`. So it goes to the `else` equivalent. This is correct.

Let's consider if `employee_info` is passed by value or reference. In Python, it's passed by reference (well, by object reference). In C++, `const EmployeeInfo&` is appropriate.

What about `list_employees`?
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID. In C++, `employee_id` is `int`, so it's wrapped in `std::variant<int, std::string>`.
Wait, `employee_details[key] = value;` where `value` is `std::variant<int, std::string>`. This works.

Is there any other way to implement `Value`?
Could `Value` be a custom class? No, `std::variant` is standard and perfectly models a dynamically typed value that can be one of a set of types.

Let's write out the full C++ code.

```cpp
#include <map>
#include <string>
#include <variant>

class HRManagementSystem {
public:
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `update_employee`, what if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

Let's consider if `get_employee` should be `const`.
In Python, `get_employee` doesn't modify `self`. However, it returns a reference to the internal dict, which *can* be modified.
In C++, if we make `get_employee` const, we can't return a non-const pointer to the internal map.
So we need two overloads of `get_employee`: one for const objects and one for non-const objects.
```cpp
    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool> get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }
```
Wait, the Python code doesn't have a const concept. Adding a const overload is idiomatic C++ and doesn't change behavior for non-const objects. I'll add it.

Wait, if I add a const overload, `GetEmployeeResult` needs to be defined for both.
```cpp
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
```
This is good.

Let's double check the `list_employees` method.
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Wait, is there any problem with `std::map` and structured bindings?
```cpp

```
This requires C++17. `std::variant` also requires C++17. So C++17 is assumed.

Let's consider the `GetEmployeeResult` type. Is there a better way?

What if we just return `EmployeeInfo*` and use `nullptr` instead of `false`?

If we return `nullptr`, the behavior of `if (hr.get_employee(1) == false)` in C++ would be `if (nullptr == false)`, which is a compile error because `nullptr` is not comparable to `false`.
Wait, `if (hr.get_employee(1) == nullptr)` would work.
But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."

```cpp
            employees.erase(employee_id);

                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
Wait, in Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Is there any edge case in `update_employee`?
What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

What about `add_employee`?
Python:
```python
    def add_employee(self, employee_id, name, position, department, salary):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return False
        else:
            self.employees[employee_id] = {
                'name': name,
                'position': position,
                'department': department,
                'salary': salary
            }
            return True
```
C++:
```cpp
    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }
```
This is identical.

What about `remove_employee`?
Python:
```python
    def remove_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False
```
C++:
```cpp
    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }
```
This is identical.

Wait, is there any case where `employee_info` in `update_employee` could have a string for `employee_id`?
No, `employee_id` is `int`.

Let's consider the `GetEmployeeResult` type. Is there a better way?
If we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`, the user has to include `<variant>`.
What if we just return `EmployeeInfo*` and use `nullptr` instead of `false`?
In Python, `get_employee` returns `False` if not found.
If we return `nullptr`, the behavior of `if (hr.get_employee(1) == false)` in C++ would be `if (nullptr == false)`, which is a compile error because `nullptr` is not comparable to `false`.
Wait, `if (hr.get_employee(1) == nullptr)` would work.
But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."
If we use `std::variant`, `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would be `hr.get_employee(1) == GetEmployeeResult(false)`, which compiles and behaves identically to Python.
However, returning a pointer to an internal map element is somewhat unidiomatic C++ (usually you'd return an iterator or `optional<reference_wrapper>`), but given the Python code returns a mutable reference or `False`, `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` is a very faithful translation.

Wait, could we use `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`?
If we return `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`, then `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would not compile because `std::optional` doesn't compare to `bool` with `==`. It can be contextually converted to `bool`, so `if (!hr.get_employee(1))` would work, but `== false` wouldn't.
In Python, `employee == False` is explicitly used in `update_employee`.
If we use `std::variant`, we can support `== false`.
Let's stick with `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`. It's the most accurate representation of the Python code's semantics.

Wait, is `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` safe?
If `EmployeeInfo*` happens to have the same value as `false` (i.e., `nullptr`), then `std::variant` holding `nullptr` would be different from `std::variant` holding `false`.
In Python, `False` is a boolean, not `None` or `0`. So `std::variant` distinguishes between `nullptr` (which would be like `None`) and `false` (which is `False`). This is perfect, because in Python, `get_employee` returns `False`, not `None`.

Let's check `update_employee` again.
```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }
```
This matches the Python logic perfectly.

Wait, in Python:
```python
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If `employee` is a dict, `employee == False` is `False`. So it goes to `else`.
In C++, `emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)` will be `false` if `emp_result` holds an `EmployeeInfo*`. So it goes to the `else` equivalent. This is correct.

Let's consider if `employee_info` is passed by value or reference. In Python, it's passed by reference (well, by object reference). In C++, `const EmployeeInfo&` is appropriate.

What about `list_employees`?
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID. In C++, `employee_id` is `int`, so it's wrapped in `std::variant<int, std::string>`.
Wait, `employee_details[key] = value;` where `value` is `std::variant<int, std::string>`. This works.

Is there any other way to implement `Value`?
Could `Value` be a custom class? No, `std::variant` is standard and perfectly models a dynamically typed value that can be one of a set of types.

Let's write out the full C++ code.

```cpp
#include <map>
#include <string>
#include <variant>

class HRManagementSystem {
public:
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `update_employee`, what if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

Let's consider if `get_employee` should be `const`.
In Python, `get_employee` doesn't modify `self`. However, it returns a reference to the internal dict, which *can* be modified.
In C++, if we make `get_employee` const, we can't return a non-const pointer to the internal map.
So we need two overloads of `get_employee`: one for const objects and one for non-const objects.
```cpp
    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }
```
Wait, the Python code doesn't have a const concept. Adding a const overload is idiomatic C++ and doesn't change behavior for non-const objects. I'll add it.

Wait, if I add a const overload, `GetEmployeeResult` needs to be defined for both.
```cpp
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
```
This is good.

Let's double check the `list_employees` method.
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Wait, is there any problem with `std::map` and structured bindings?
```cpp
        for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
```
This requires C++17. `std::variant` also requires C++17. So C++17 is assumed.

```cpp
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {

        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }

        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;

        return true;

        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {

            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {

                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```

Wait, the Python code doesn't have a const concept. Adding a const overload is idiomatic C++ and doesn't change behavior for non-const objects. I'll add it.

Wait, if I add a const overload, `GetEmployeeResult` needs to be defined for both.
```cpp
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
```
This is good.

Let's double check the `list_employees` method.
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Wait, is there any problem with `std::map` and structured bindings?
```cpp
        for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
```
This requires C++17. `std::variant` also requires C++17. So C++17 is assumed.

Is there any edge case in `add_employee`?
Python:
```python
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return False
        else:
            self.employees[employee_id] = {
                'name': name,
                'position': position,
                'department': department,
                'salary': salary
            }
            return True
```
C++:
```cpp
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
```

Python:
```python
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
```
This is identical.

Wait, what if `employee_info` in `update_employee` is empty?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If empty, loops don't run, returns `True`.
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
```
If empty, loops don't run, returns `true`. Identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

Let's consider the `Value` type again.
Could we use `std::any` instead of `std::variant<int, std::string>`?
`std::any` is slower and requires casting, but it's more flexible. However, the Python code only uses `int` and `str` for values. `std::variant<int, std::string>` is type-safe and sufficient.

Let's review `list_employees`:
```python
    def list_employees(self):
        employee_data = {}
        if self.employees:
            for employee_id, employee_info in self.employees.items():
                employee_details = {}
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id
                for key, value in employee_info.items():
                    employee_details[key] = value
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details
        return employee_data
```
In C++:
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
Wait, in Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Is there any edge case in `update_employee`?
What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

What about `add_employee`?
Python:
```python
    def add_employee(self, employee_id, name, position, department, salary):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            return False
        else:
            self.employees[employee_id] = {
                'name': name,
                'position': position,
                'department': department,
                'salary': salary
            }
            return True
```
C++:
```cpp
    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }
```
This is identical.

What about `remove_employee`?
Python:
```python
    def remove_employee(self, employee_id):
        if employee_id in self.employees:
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False
```
C++:
```cpp
    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }
```
This is identical.

Wait, is there any case where `employee_info` in `update_employee` could have a string for `employee_id`?
No, `employee_id` is `int`.

Let's consider the `GetEmployeeResult` type. Is there a better way?
If we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`, the user has to include `<variant>`.
What if we just return `EmployeeInfo*` and use `nullptr` instead of `false`?
In Python, `get_employee` returns `False` if not found.
If we return `nullptr`, the behavior of `if (hr.get_employee(1) == false)` in C++ would be `if (nullptr == false)`, which is a compile error because `nullptr` is not comparable to `false`.
Wait, `if (hr.get_employee(1) == nullptr)` would work.
But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."
If we use `std::variant`, `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would be `hr.get_employee(1) == GetEmployeeResult(false)`, which compiles and behaves identically to Python.
However, returning a pointer to an internal map element is somewhat unidiomatic C++ (usually you'd return an iterator or `optional<reference_wrapper>`), but given the Python code returns a mutable reference or `False`, `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` is a very faithful translation.

Wait, could we use `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`?
If we return `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`, then `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would not compile because `std::optional` doesn't compare to `bool` with `==`. It can be contextually converted to `bool`, so `if (!hr.get_employee(1))` would work, but `== false` wouldn't.
In Python, `employee == False` is explicitly used in `update_employee`.
If we use `std::variant`, we can support `== false`.
Let's stick with `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`. It's the most accurate representation of the Python code's semantics.

Wait, is `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` safe?
If `EmployeeInfo*` happens to have the same value as `false` (i.e., `nullptr`), then `std::variant` holding `nullptr` would be different from `std::variant` holding `false`.
In Python, `False` is a boolean, not `None` or `0`. So `std::variant` distinguishes between `nullptr` (which would be like `None`) and `false` (which is `False`). This is perfect, because in Python, `get_employee` returns `False`, not `None`.

Let's check `update_employee` again.
```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }
```
This matches the Python logic perfectly.

Wait, in Python:
```python
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If `employee` is a dict, `employee == False` is `False`. So it goes to `else`.
In C++, `emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)` will be `false` if `emp_result` holds an `EmployeeInfo*`. So it goes to the `else` equivalent. This is correct.

Let's consider if `employee_info` is passed by value or reference. In Python, it's passed by reference (well, by object reference). In C++, `const EmployeeInfo&` is appropriate.

What about `list_employees`?
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID. In C++, `employee_id` is `int`, so it's wrapped in `std::variant<int, std::string>`.
Wait, `employee_details[key] = value;` where `value` is `std::variant<int, std::string>`. This works.

Is there any other way to implement `Value`?
Could `Value` be a custom class? No, `std::variant` is standard and perfectly models a dynamically typed value that can be one of a set of types.

Let's write out the full C++ code.

```cpp
#include <map>
#include <string>
#include <variant>

class HRManagementSystem {
public:
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `update_employee`, what if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

Let's consider if `get_employee` should be `const`.
In Python, `get_employee` doesn't modify `self`. However, it returns a reference to the internal dict, which *can* be modified.
In C++, if we make `get_employee` const, we can't return a non-const pointer to the internal map.
So we need two overloads of `get_employee`: one for const objects and one for non-const objects.
```cpp
    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }
```
Wait, the Python code doesn't have a const concept. Adding a const overload is idiomatic C++ and doesn't change behavior for non-const objects. I'll add it.

Wait, if I add a const overload, `GetEmployeeResult` needs to be defined for both.
```cpp
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
```
This is good.

Let's double check the `list_employees` method.
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

```cpp
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `update_employee`, what if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?
Python:
```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
```
C++:
```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
```
This is identical.

Let's consider if `get_employee` should be `const`.
In Python, `get_employee` doesn't modify `self`. However, it returns a reference to the internal dict, which *can* be modified.
In C++, if we make `get_employee` const, we can't return a non-const pointer to the internal map.
So we need two overloads of `get_employee`: one for const objects and one for non-const objects.
```cpp
    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }
```
Wait, the Python code doesn't have a const concept. Adding a const overload is idiomatic C++ and doesn't change behavior for non-const objects. I'll add it.

Wait, if I add a const overload, `GetEmployeeResult` needs to be defined for both.
```cpp
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
```
This is good.

Let's double check the `list_employees` method.
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Wait, is there any problem with `std::map` and structured bindings?
```cpp
        for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
```
This requires C++17. `std::variant` also requires C++17. So C++17 is assumed.

Wait, what if `employee_info` in `update_employee` is empty?

```python
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If empty, loops don't run, returns `True`.

```cpp
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }

            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
```
If empty, loops don't run, returns `true`. Identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

Let's consider the `Value` type again.
Could we use `std::any` instead of `std::variant<int, std::string>`?
`std::any` is slower and requires casting, but it's more flexible. However, the Python code only uses `int` and `str` for values. `std::variant<int, std::string>` is type-safe and sufficient.

Let's review `list_employees`:
```python
    def list_employees(self):
        employee_data = {}
        if self.employees:
            for employee_id, employee_info in self.employees.items():
                employee_details = {}
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id
                for key, value in employee_info.items():
                    employee_details[key] = value
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details
        return employee_data
```
In C++:
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
Wait, in Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID to the dictionary.
In C++, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;` will assign `employee_id` (which is `int`) to a `Value` (which is `std::variant<int, std::string>`). This works perfectly.

Is there any edge case in `update_employee`?

```python
            del self.employees[employee_id]
            return True
        else:
            return False
```
C++:
```cpp
    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
```

This is identical.

Wait, is there any case where `employee_info` in `update_employee` could have a string for `employee_id`?
No, `employee_id` is `int`.

Let's consider the `GetEmployeeResult` type. Is there a better way?
If we use `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`, the user has to include `<variant>`.
What if we just return `EmployeeInfo*` and use `nullptr` instead of `false`?
In Python, `get_employee` returns `False` if not found.
If we return `nullptr`, the behavior of `if (hr.get_employee(1) == false)` in C++ would be `if (nullptr == false)`, which is a compile error because `nullptr` is not comparable to `false`.
Wait, `if (hr.get_employee(1) == nullptr)` would work.
But the prompt says "Keep behavior identical (inputs/outputs, side effects, edge cases, exceptions). Use idiomatic C++ only when it doesn't change behavior."
If we use `std::variant`, `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would be `hr.get_employee(1) == GetEmployeeResult(false)`, which compiles and behaves identically to Python.
However, returning a pointer to an internal map element is somewhat unidiomatic C++ (usually you'd return an iterator or `optional<reference_wrapper>`), but given the Python code returns a mutable reference or `False`, `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` is a very faithful translation.

Wait, could we use `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`?
If we return `std::optional<std::reference_wrapper<EmployeeInfo>>`, then `hr.get_employee(1) == false` would not compile because `std::optional` doesn't compare to `bool` with `==`. It can be contextually converted to `bool`, so `if (!hr.get_employee(1))` would work, but `== false` wouldn't.
In Python, `employee == False` is explicitly used in `update_employee`.
If we use `std::variant`, we can support `== false`.
Let's stick with `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>`. It's the most accurate representation of the Python code's semantics.

Wait, is `std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>` safe?
If `EmployeeInfo*` happens to have the same value as `false` (i.e., `nullptr`), then `std::variant` holding `nullptr` would be different from `std::variant` holding `false`.
In Python, `False` is a boolean, not `None` or `0`. So `std::variant` distinguishes between `nullptr` (which would be like `None`) and `false` (which is `False`). This is perfect, because in Python, `get_employee` returns `False`, not `None`.

Let's check `update_employee` again.
```cpp
    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }
```
This matches the Python logic perfectly.

Wait, in Python:
```python
        employee = self.get_employee(employee_id)
        if employee == False:
            return False
        else:
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                if key not in employee:
                    return False
            for key, value in employee_info.items():
                employee[key] = value
            return True
```
If `employee` is a dict, `employee == False` is `False`. So it goes to `else`.
In C++, `emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)` will be `false` if `emp_result` holds an `EmployeeInfo*`. So it goes to the `else` equivalent. This is correct.

Let's consider if `employee_info` is passed by value or reference. In Python, it's passed by reference (well, by object reference). In C++, `const EmployeeInfo&` is appropriate.

What about `list_employees`?
```cpp
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
```
In Python, `employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id` adds the ID. In C++, `employee_id` is `int`, so it's wrapped in `std::variant<int, std::string>`.
Wait, `employee_details[key] = value;` where `value` is `std::variant<int, std::string>`. This works.

Is there any other way to implement `Value`?
Could `Value` be a custom class? No, `std::variant` is standard and perfectly models a dynamically typed value that can be one of a set of types.

Let's write out the full C++ code.

```cpp
#include <map>
#include <string>
#include <variant>

class HRManagementSystem {
public:
    using Value = std::variant<int, std::string>;
    using EmployeeInfo = std::map<std::string, Value>;
    using GetEmployeeResult = std::variant<EmployeeInfo*, bool>;
    using ConstGetEmployeeResult = std::variant<const EmployeeInfo*, bool>;

private:
    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employees;

public:
    HRManagementSystem() = default;

    bool add_employee(int employee_id, const std::string& name, const std::string& position, const std::string& department, int salary) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            return false;
        }
        employees[employee_id] = {
            {"name", name},
            {"position", position},
            {"department", department},
            {"salary", salary}
        };
        return true;
    }

    bool remove_employee(int employee_id) {
        if (employees.find(employee_id) != employees.end()) {
            employees.erase(employee_id);
            return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    bool update_employee(int employee_id, const EmployeeInfo& employee_info) {
        GetEmployeeResult emp_result = get_employee(employee_id);
        if (emp_result == GetEmployeeResult(false)) {
            return false;
        }
        EmployeeInfo* employee = std::get<EmployeeInfo*>(emp_result);
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            if (employee->find(key) == employee->end()) {
                return false;
            }
        }
        for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
            (*employee)[key] = value;
        }
        return true;
    }

    GetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    ConstGetEmployeeResult get_employee(int employee_id) const {
        auto it = employees.find(employee_id);
        if (it != employees.end()) {
            return &(it->second);
        }
        return false;
    }

    std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> list_employees() const {
        std::map<int, EmployeeInfo> employee_data;
        if (!employees.empty()) {
            for (const auto& [employee_id, employee_info] : employees) {
                EmployeeInfo employee_details;
                employee_details["employee_ID"] = employee_id;
                for (const auto& [key, value] : employee_info) {
                    employee_details[key] = value;
                }
                employee_data[employee_id] = employee_details;
            }
        }
        return employee_data;
    }
};
```

Wait, in `update_employee`, what if `employee_info` contains a key that is in `employee`, but the value type is different?
In Python, types are dynamic, so `employee[key] = value` just overwrites.
In C++, `(*employee)[key] = value;` will overwrite the `Value` variant with the new variant. This is identical.

What if `employee_info` contains a key that is not in `employee`?

```cpp
        }
```
